Lithium ion batteries are the first choice for any electronics where it's important to pack the maximum amount of energy in to the smallest space possible BUT that also makes them dangerous. There have been numerous fires caused by these batteries, not only in aircraft, but also in people's homes (eg. electric scooters). To make matters worse, they don't need a supply of external oxygen to burn - they have all they need inside them. To operate safely, the charging and discharging rates need to be carefully controlled, which is why it's important to only buy battery systems from top brand suppliers who have the engineering resources to design them properly. Even then, top brand products have been known to catch fire and when they do, the resultant chain reaction can result in an explosion. This is why they should never be packed in people's hold luggage. If you have them with you in the cabin and one catches fire, then the cabin crew know how to deal with it, but if it's in the hold ... well, there's no chance.

It's also important for anyone that is shipping things to know the rules about what needs to be labeled as containing lithium batteries and hazardous goods. These items will likely have a surcharge for special handling. In most cases your cheapest option is just going to be shipping ground and not express services. FWIW, USPS Priority mail is considered express and is largely handled by FedEx.
People don't understand how dangerous batteries, especially new high capacity ones are.

These lithium batteries are soo important for our life nowadays, but also still soo dangerous. Hope there will be some development.  I use these batterie cella for my scuba dive lamps. Thats just the way to go today, if you want lightweight and compact equipmenty while having maximal burn time and brightness. Hence I need from time to time take these with me by plane for a dive trip. I take them with roughly only charged to about 25 to 40%,  so less energy (discharging more is no good for the battery...). Each battery is in an extra plastic transport container with a sealant, so no liquid can enter. Additionally I put tape on the poles and put them in another plastic container in my hand luggage. Chances they are getting crushed or short cutted are minimized. They are usually allowed.  But actually smartphone batteries have a much higher capacity. And they are used and more likely to fall in between the seats. Also laptop batteries are much bigger...
It is also important to not lie or omit information about cargo shipments. Sadly this still happens. Please do not do this, and do not allow your employer to pressure you into doing so, regardless of the reason they give.

There are No Fire Suppression Systems Available for Lithium Battery Fires.  Early Detection is your Only Hope. These are not Normal Fires.  The Problem isn't just that they produce their own Oxygen. These Battery Fires are ReDox Fires (Reduction/Oxidation).  These are Literally Electrical Fires. The Next Scary thing We will have to learn About are 'Coulombic Explosions'.  Ever see Sodium Metal Explode in Water?  There you go...

I believe that now lithium batteries are no longer allowed on cargo planes.
Having prevkously worked as an x-ray operator for cargo I can testify that a lot of people try to ship Li-ion batteries without declaring them as DG. Holding parcels due to undeclared DG was pretty much a routine part of the day when I worked there.  I worked for a security firm that had a contract to do the x-ray screening on behalf of the carrier, and the good thing about that carrier was that part of the contract you sign with them when posting a shipment is that you allow for the opening of the shipment for manual/visual inspection. So I could just pull any shipment aside and open it up to confirm the contents, and when in doubt we just opened it,  and seeing that it did contain DG, the shipment was halted so they could contact the sender. I don't know but I think there might be a combination of people not knowing/reading as well as some trying to avoid the extra charge of shipping dangerous goods.  Anyway, we took our job seriously to prevent incidents like this.

That thermal runaway was likely caused by lack of pressurization in the cargo compartment which can cause lithium batteries to distort and fail into thermal runaway. The main reason (other than to keep them with the passengers so if there is a problem it can be dealt with) I suspect it is forbidden with commercial flights to allow passengers to put lithium battery devices into checked luggage is for this same reason - luggage holds on most commercial planes are not pressurized. As the aircraft climbs to cruise level the drop in pressure can cause them to distort and fail.
Dad flew cargo in the 1950s. His cargo burned, the entire fuselage became involved, and they lost all electronics at night coming in over mountains in Central America. The crew landed the aircraft and were rescued via the cockpit windows.
I feel like there really should be way more anti-fire systems in cargo holds, cargo fires have been a perennial issue since we were shipping goods with sail power and systems like the ones here are well below what we're technologically capable of now

I am working in IT and so when I travel I have to take a reasonable amount of devices with batteries with me (phones, power banks, ...) and at some point I decided it would be better to just put all of them into a lunchbox. Despite all the good ideas that came from this, I'm surprised that a much simpler solution had not been suggested: VOICE COMMAND CONTROL AND MONITORING. Press button on headset to set voice command. Voice repeats what it thinks you said. Longpress to accept.   Why it's an easy suggestion: -It's a very simple technology, that has been around in simple command for sixty damn years. That's longer than it took to get from a plane hopping a field to putting a man in space. -Additionally the microphone is in the pilot's face, and the headphones are on his ears, there is no radio interference, so there would be less noise to filter than on a regular CVR. -With the system being button and pilot mic activated it would not be a security risk. -The computational power needed is so minimal it could fit on a single chip, or at worst a single circuit board. -Since it would require confirmation after each command no accidental commands could be issued. -It wouldn't represent any major changes to the way the aircraft flies, as it would only be a secondary input method for all regular inputs. -It would need only minimal training as all the handling characteristics and procedures would stay the same, just now that it can be done without actually pressing down on some button. -In a fault the system could be turned off and the manual buttons used. -And of course it would be cheap, because with all instrumentation and inputs running through the computer nowadays anyway, it would only require a software update. -As a backup, and in case of fast reactions needed the standard inputs would be available anyway. -We have seen it for decades in sci-fi everyone is perfectly ready for this technology. Computer, warp 5. -Even older large aircraft these days have some sort of flybywire system, even if it's just autopilot, and connecting this into the autopilot systems would be easy.  As for why it would actually be a good thing: -It would automatically take care of verification to both pilots, combating accidents where values are set accidentally. The number of deaths caused by some flight control being accidentally bumped is way WAY too high. -It would take care of callouts that keep the pilots unnecessarily busy. There have also been routinely issues with these not being followed which has also lead to accidents. -It could alert the pilots if the pilot monitoring fails to check the value it calls out. Thereby preventing system faults going undetected because failed checking on the secondary system. Which has lead to numerous accidents. -It could in an emergency call out checklist items and pull them on screen. The fact that pilots still need to manually search and click on the checklist they need even in the rare case that digital checklists are supported, and worse most often have to flip through ACTUAL PHYSICAL PAGES that can get DROPPED, like frikkin carved stone tablets, is absolutely unbelievable in the 21st century. This has caused so many damn accidents, even though checklists are there to prevent them. -It could also alert the pilots to checklist items overlooked, which ALSO caused accidents. -It would serve as a calming clear guidance to any overloaded/semi incapacitated pilot preventing degradation of situational awareness. Which would have prevented some accidents. -It would enable someone with unclear grasp of the plane type (new pilot/crewmember with incapacitated pilots) to land the plane alone, which would have prevented a few accidents in and of itself. -It would enable clarity about the state of critical systems in case the pilots miss it during scanning. Landing configuration, autopilot disconnect, fire, overspeed, stall, and too low callouts are all fine, but these seem like a fairly arbitrary choice when something like an unresponsive trim, an idle thrust lever, brakes not engaged, and overheating engines are all also things that can be just as fatal if not controlled equally soon, and might go unnoticed for too long a time. -It would finally also enable a bit of control and feedback in case the cockpit becomes filled with smoke. This is clearly a large enough issue that there are several systems to supply air to the cockpit in such a case and to vent the cockpit and even to even design ridiculously cumbersome inflatable tubes so that the pilots may see what's in front of them.   Most accidents covered on your channel could have been avoided with this system.
This one brought me to tears. A trend i keep deducing from all the analysis you've done on so many flights is that while you should always second guess yourself, and your training, *you shouldn't ever do half measures*. In this case it was -Having a critical emergency but not landing at the nearest airport but at the nearest convenient airport. -Not descending to the lowest safe altitude as fast as possible once descent was programmed in. -Verify your inputs into the autopilot if it's the only system helping you. -Do not disconnect the autopilot if it's the only system able to control the aircraft. -If you take manual control take control of everything, including the thrust.  Even the last thing could have saved at least one life. The plane was still flyable and almost even manually controllable even several minutes after the indicated missed landing, so the fire wasn't getting worse. And this was with low speed, so with higher speed the FO might have been able to control it better and somehow made it to the other airport. Or since he was turned almost fully back, he might have been able to come in for another attempt, this time with a lower altitude and speed. Without autoland, gears, runway visibility, and bad flight display visibility it would have been a bad landing, but it would've left better chances of survival than dropping out of the sky due to idle thrust. Or with so much desert around he could have just put it down in the desert going by the altitude callouts alone.

I have a question that will hopefully be answered, fully aware that this video is over a year old.   What would be the limiting factors that precludes the possibility of having a ram air system to clear smoke from the cockpit of a freighter like this. Since the aircraft was depressurized, why can't a small door in front of the cockpit be opened to allow the forward movement of the plane to force fresh air into the cockpit and a door in the back of the plane be opened to allow the contaminated air be forced out the rear of the plane?  I'm not talking about a big door, but simply a forward facing air vent that leads directly into the front of the cockpit. That seems like it would create a much higher pressure in the cockpit due to forward motion and the open vent in the tail would be a natural place for the bad air and smoke to be driven out, clearing the plane as it goes.   The vent shown in the video appears to be in top of the plane, which normally would suck the smoke out, but let's face it, emergencies are emergencies largely because they are NOT normal. A forward and rear facing vent would not have a choice but to work, providing both can be opened.

Just a quick correction. You keep stating "lithium batteries", when it's lithium ion batteries that are so energy dense and have very low internal resistance (meaning they can supply huge amounts of current when shorted, high end modern lithium ion batteries can already have sustained outputs of 30A - this + brushless motors are why battery tools are stupidly powerful compared to how weak they were in just the 2000s).  Lithium batteries have been around for a lot longer and are very safe. They have a similar energy density to alkaline batteries. Except the benefit of lithium AA batteries over alkaline batteries is that the voltage doesn't slowly drop off. It'll stay at the rated 1.5V until it's nearly out, when it'll rapidly drop. So it's brilliant for some applications which like a high voltage, but it also means that AA/AAA devices that show you the battery % will give inaccurate readings, they'll be at 100% for ages then suddenly the device will just turn off.
Obviously it's dangerous to have a cargo hold right under the controls

New Boeing aircraft have also been modified so that 'buttons' on the panels are now recessed slightly when in the depressed position. Prior to this accident  when a button was depressed it was flush with the panel and, therefore, there was no tactile way of knowing where the button was in relation to the panel. Now, with it slightly recessed, the pilot can feel where a particular button is: in other words the button can be felt in the 'off' position because it sticks out; it can be felt in the 'on' position because it is recessed into the panel.  In the military we were trained to do blind-cockpit-checks in the event we couldn't actually see the panel we had to know where each button and switch was, and what position it was in, by feel. With the new recessed buttons in the Boeing planes that advantage has again been given back to the pilots.

This makes a lot of sense. Depressurizing a self-oxidizing fire will definitely make things worse as there will be less air to take the heat away. Normal fires never get very hot because they are constantly shedding their heat into their surroundings, mostly the air (flames). If you take away the air the only place the energy can go is straight down. So it just burned hotter and hotter, concentrating all that heat into the fire lining. Basically thermite

Why can't there be a requirement for lithium batteries to be stored in approved containers? The RC hobby industry recognizes the danger of lithium batteries and there are numerous containers sold specifically for the safe storage, transport and use of lithium battery packs which are standard for all types of RC hobby vehicles, aircraft and vessels. I have an aircraft technician's license and worked in an aviation repair station that specialized in aviation batteries. I was in the battery business for over 30 years.  I was out of the repair side early on and for the most part of 25 years trained other technicians at commercial airlines and major repair stations throughout north and south America. Just at the end of my stint in the battery market, around 2006, the talk was all about lithium batteries in the aviation industry. This turned into a new learning venture as I knew I had to be well versed on the subject. I ended up on a panel, more as an observer, on the use of these batteries in different systems on the aircraft. Including main power.  Although the technology has improved dramatically since then for the ones used in the aircraft, the lithium batteries inside the aircraft brought on by passengers and crew members is not something to take lightly people.  I have been saying for years that it's only a matter of time that a huge restriction would be trigger by a lithium fire. While lithium batteries are indeed scary, even plain AA batteries can misbehave if mistreated.  Our daughter-in-law was in line at a checkout counter when our grandson noticed smoke coming from her purse.  Then it caught fire.  Frantically opening the purse, she discovered a single alkaline AA battery had gotten jumbled with stray coins in the bottom of her purse.  And thru the normal jostling you would expect, a temporary short-circuit occurred, tying the positive and negative ends together.  Dumping the contents on the floor, she saw the battery was smoking hot and splitting wide open.  Moral of  the story?  Every methodology we humans design for storing, using and concentrating  energy, (batteries, camp fires, gasoline tankers, nuclear reactors, . . . ), they all can go astray rapidly if not tended to properly.  As a former road warrior with 40+ years of field service work throughout the US and Canada, I learned LONG ago.  ALL batteries in storage, alive or dead,  are either in a individual plastic bag, or have at least one terminal taped over.  Usually both.  Tape is cheap.  Lives are not.

Important info about batteries: If your device (e.g. laptop, phone) starts "bulging", have it repaired immediately and until then, treat it like a time bomb. If you can't see it bulge/crack, but the battery life suddenly drops ("I thought I had just charged it!") then it's the same thing.  Get the battery replaced (if you know how, you can do it yourself) and safely and immediately dispose of the old one. There has to be some efficient way of enabling the cargo planes to take the necessary actions automatically, once the internal sensors in the plane and in cargo containers, pick up the minimal traces of any fires. For example, containers carrying batteries and other high risk items, would have the appropriate fire extinguishing recourses, to extinguish a fire provoked by those cargo items. If any fire starts outside the containers, the extinguishing resources and maneuvers necessary to be starved of oxygen, are activated with very little interaction from pilots. By now, measures like these should have been implemented since that described incident took place.

'make sure that the batteries you carry are allowed to be carried on passanger aircrafts"  that brings me back to when I had to get off a flight due to my wheelchair batttery not being suitable to fly, and unfortunatly wasnt noticed (by anyone, including myself) until i was literally on the plane I tried to do the right thing, when i brought it i told the sales person and the occupational theapist that it needed to be able to go on planes, I also contacted the dangerous good department of the airline and got approval through them, and then went through it all again at check in. That's so sad. Lithium-ion battery fires are extremely dangerous because they are self-oxidising. If you see a laptop NMC type cell fire, it's like a solid fuel rocket engine. Normal fire extinguisers cannot put them out. Depressurising the hold may even provoke the cells to expand and deform, setting them off due to differential pressure in the sealed cell. The 'Dreamliner' APU battery fires were also lithium-ion and caused by poor battery selection choice (they were an unstable type) and bad battery charger design. They had to ground the whole fleet.

This story was very scary. In my previous job I worked with electric scooters and other small personal electric vehicles (PEVs). Well done.. an amazing depiction of that accident.. I really did feel for the crew. Before moving into professional flying I had a very long career as a professional yacht skipper. On one occasion, 130 miles from land on  large motor yacht, we suffered a major fire. I'd undergone training for these events.. and later as a pilot, many more training events.. In spite of being warned many times of the speed that fire can progress, it still came as a shock.. this was a very large motor yacht, the fire suppression system had failed and in spite of multiple attempts to deal with the fire, choking, nearly blind, burning lungs.. we had to abandon the yacht in less than 30 minutes. Many years later, having retired from professional flying, I continued as an instructor. I was amazed that the onboard fire checklist pertaining to the aircraft on which I was instructing, detailed multiple attempts at troubleshooting the problem while still at altitude. I always emphasised the importance of descending, diverting to the nearest available airport, and troubleshooting with the airport immediately available. Better troubleshoot with the airport in sight than at 35,000 feet,

The battery rules are really vague and don't restrict much. A battery is two or more cells connected together to increase either voltage or capacity. But recently (The FAA as well) has been including single cells in this category.   The rule is that you can't carry batteries over a set capacity of 100 watt hours. However, you may carry as many as you like. So all you need to do is make a battery with cells below 100 watt hours, and switch off the connection between each cell before boarding. I can pack a max size carryon with batteries and it is perfectly allowed, yet VERY dangerous.

Bedankt
There was a loss of an aircraft due to an in-flight cargo fire and subsequent crash, that of ValuJet Airlines Flight 592 near Miami, Florida, on May 11, 1996 which was caused by the Airlines Maintenence company Sabretech. They had shipped a consignment of chemical oxygen generators that had expired dates on them, from their maintenace depot on one of their commercial aircraft. The generators had safety pins in to stop them operating, but they were incorrectly secured and the boxes they were in were mislabelled as them being "empty" - they were not. They started operating and because they produce oxygen and get very hot (as it's an exothermic reaction), they started and sustained a very hot fire. The plane crashed, killing all 110 onboard. It's not just passengers that can be responsible for starting an onboard fire.

Gut wrenching.  Here's something to make your hair stand on end.  Kohls handles returns for Amazon.  When processing these returns, if there are hazardous items, the system will alert us and we're to tape a hazmat label to the box in which this hazardous item is placed.  These boxes are palletized and taken to the local Amazon distribution center.  From there, they are flown to a centralized hub for disposal or additional handling.  One of the haz labels was for lithium batteries.  Sometimes we'd get an alert that the item we were returning was hazardous, but the system did not tell us which label to put on the box.  Also, they used pieces of paper for these haz labels rather than adhesive backed stickers, so they often got ripped off the boxes during handling.  The Kohls location I'm familiar with was doing 300-400 returns a day.  Kohls has 1900 locations.  So that's a lot of boxes containing potentially hazardous material being shipped around with a very loosey goosey process to identify them.  After seeing this video...wow.  The Kohls scene is just ONE of perhaps endless possibilities of hazardous product being put on planes without the airport handling team knowing it. It would seem like requiring lithium batteries on freight flights to be packed in a dry powder extinguishing agent suitable for lithium fires, such as a copper-sand blend, would be a reasonable step to reduce risk. This would reduce shipping efficiency on such batteries, but would also ensure that an extinguishing agent is available as soon as a fire starts to spread. Similar methods have been used in ammunition storage with "Wet" storage, where ammunition is stored in a container within a water jacket to reduce ignition chances.

As a careful, responsible and professional drone pilot - If I'm to take my drone onboard an aircraft, I always check the batteries in as park of my "Hand luggage".  Here is my safety procedure:  1. Check with my airline that my battery type is allowed onboard the aircraft.  2. Also check the "maximum' number of batteries I can bring onboard  - this is normally set by a total batter capacity rating, or mili-amp hour (mAh).  This max limit, divided over a battery packs maximum capacity, equals total number of batteries permitted. 3 - fit terminal covers - I do this as a matter of course, to prevent potential exposure to dust, durt and moisture. 4 - Put batteries in individual fire safety bags (lipo bags) - this is a very easy and inexpensive way to demonstrate to the airline your flying with, that your know what your doing and have taken steps to limit a potential risk onboard their aircraft.  5 - Never use "lipo bags" that can carry multiple batteries - A truly bad idea indeed! Lipo bags prevent fire from spreading beyond the bag, but these type don't prevent any fire spread within the bag! So a fire in one battery will quickly spread to the other battery, making the situation worse! 6 - Drain the power down prior! - this is the key and most important step. NEVER, travel with a fully charged lipo battery! A lipo fire is a chemical reaction gone wrong.  The best and easiest way to prevent it is to travel with the least amount of power within them.  Provides the maximum amount of space for those atoms to flow and the least amount of fuel for the fire to use if a fire should occur.   You can always charge them afterwards and it's another way of showing their save - by pressing the power button and showing power indicator lights on the batteries.  Fly safe everyone!
Cargo pilots should have parachutes, what's the worst thing that could happen? Lose some cargo and a plane?
Sadly, there is no fire suppression system capable of extinguishing lithium battery fires.  The degree of fire depends on the level of charge the batteries originally had.  Lithium-Ion batteries burn like Thermite and nothing will put them out until they have expended their energy.  They also burn at temperatures much like Thermite (thousands of degrees F), easily melting aluminum and steel.  Some electric vehicle fires have burned for up to a week.  The reason why phones and laptops have such a long battery life, is because of the great amount of energy Lithium-Ion batteries store.  If that energy is released in the form of a fire, then you are in a world of hurt.  I honestly believe there is no liner or suppression system capable of controlling a battery fire.  They are just too hot and have their own oxidizer.  The best way to control a Lithium Ion battery fire is to dump the burning cargo in the ocean, if that were possible.

I must admit, I'm surprised that in an emergency like this, ATC doesn't have the capability (authority?) to have one controller tune one radio to what the airplane is stuck on (in this case, the Bahrain ATC frequency).  I know why this would not normally be the case - in the airspace between the two, there would be planes that are probably able to get some signal from both, which could create interference, but I would expect this to be a manageable annoyance.   Sure you don't want that, but it's a lot better to have that than having a 6 stage round trip communication method with the pilot whose captain is passed out/dead on the floor and who's trapped in a cabin that's so full of smoke he can't see most of the controls and instruments.  Or I'm underestimating the amount of interference that would cause and doing this would create an enormous risk to every plane in the Bahrain ATC's field of operations.  My knowledge of how to use radios is largely limited to knowing about how far you can go from the "leaky feeder" radio lines in an underground mine before you get cut off.
Regarding the flight control cables: you wouldn't necessarily even need to disrupt the system that holds the cables in order to change the tension, unless there's a system involved that continuously monitors and adjusts tension to its proper balance point when the yoke is not in use.  This is because of thermal expansion.  The exact effects depend on a variety of factors: the length of the heated object, the change in temperature of that object, and the material it's made of.  (Cable would probably also have a different coefficient of expansion than a solid bar of the same material.)  For solid steel (about 10^-5 coefficient of thermal expansion depending on the alloy), 5 meters of heated area and 500 degrees increase in temperature, the effect would only be about 2.5cm, but given steel isn't particularly stretchy, that can have a massive effect on the tension force being applied.  Indeed, if you run the numbers using a Young's Modulus of 200 GPa (about midrange for steel alloys), you get about 5 GPa of pressure relief due to that expansion.  All of these numbers have been kinda rough assumptions, but they're based in a degree of logic (lithium ion fires are hot, that crew quarters section that matched the width of the lithium ion battery section is probably around 5 meters long, most cables are made of *some* kind of steel), but now I'm going to have to make a very rough guess at the cross sectional area of the cables - I'm going to assume they're about an inch in diameter, which gives a cross sectional area of about 4 square centimetres, which is 0.0004 square meters - multiplying that by the pressure relief of 5 GPa from earlier and you get about 2 million Newtons of tensile force that's just gone.  That's...about enough force to lift 200 metric tonnes.  That's...probably way more than the amount of tension these cables were under in the first place.  Note that all of these calculations are very imprecise - I'm assuming the cables are steel, of an alloy that is about midrange for steels in both thermal expansion and elastic modulus, I'm assuming the length of cable subjected to heating and the average heating of that length of cable.  I'm assuming nothing relevant to this system just *melted*.  And I'm assuming the thickness of the cable purely based on the idea that it probably takes a fair bit of force to move flight control surfaces against the wind at the speed these planes move, and thus the cable needs to be fairly strong.  But the physics principles aren't made up, they're a real issue.  (If you ever look at a modern bridge, you'll notice joints near where the span connects to the surface roads where there's room for the entire bridge span to expand and contract with the temperature fluctuations of the year, this is exactly so bridges don't break themselves on a hot day...that's the most obvious example of thermal expansion being accounted for in engineering)  And I'm reasonably confident that this back of envelope pile of assumptions and calculations is close enough to accurate that I'm on the right track.  (This also doesn't account for the fact that when materials heat up, they become easier to deform.  (See blacksmithing for an extreme example.)  I wouldn't be at all surprised that heating the cable would have also increased the amount it will stretch with the same amount of force, providing further relief of tension.  However, I don't know the physics of how to calculate that aspect, and I'm confident the thermal expansion alone was sufficient to relieve the tension on the cable entirely based on the above caclulations.)

17:25   "Cables" are most likely STEEL or another metal ... and any metal will have lower tensile strength at "a few hundred degrees". This is why THE JET FUEL ON 9/11 DID NOT HAVE TO "MELT" THE STEEL BEAMS OF THE TWIN TOWERS ... they just reduced the mechanical strength of the material to the point where it could no longer bear the load. "Less tension" = the cables probably lengthened due to that lowered resistance, which ultimately reduces the tension of the cables.
13:20   Concerning "using pressure differences": When you are cleaning an old (and LARGE) building of Asbestos ... you "lightly tape the windows shut" using plastic foil (it does NOT have to be tight!) and then install a big vaccum pump somewhere which sucks out the air and creates a SLIGHTLY lower pressure inside the building. This will allow workers to keep working and prevent any of the dangerous asbestos fibres from escaping out the windows ... since the air will come only in one direction - outside to inside - due to the pressure difference.   The big vacuum pump sends the air pumped out through a big filter to catch the asbestos particles.

Once flew from Egypt to Germany, i had 4 18650 cells for a deive light in my carry-on in a plastic case such that they are isolated and protected from mechanical damage. The aiport security personell had me put them into my check-in baggage... My girlfriend passed security right behind me, the same guy looked at her pack of 18650is in the exact same case and put it back into her carry-on.. I was well aware of the dangers of Lithium Batteries in checked-in baggage, but did not want to complain, as the security in egypt is not well known for their friendliness.. The 18650 i had have a battery chemistry which is considered "safe".. In the case they where in, they probably would not start a fire even if thermal runaway happens..   Other passengers had their powerbanks taken away from them (probably they needed them?!) The airline had a policy that batteries up to 70Wh (or up to XYZ for required medical devices) are ok in the carry-on, however explain that to a egyptian security guard which needs a power bank and has no problem in making you miss your flight..  Think their should be more awarness for the severe effects such batteries can have.. People probably but devices with batteries into their checked-in luggage daily, like electric razors.. The pressure difference between ground and the ~750mbar in the cabin when flying at travelheight, could be just enough to pop an already damaged / outgased pouch cell with a "non-safe" chemistry.. Wonder if there are more precautions for passenger luggage in regards to fire safety (like having only XY amount of burnable material in one container + a way to handle the heat + smoke).

Tanks and equipment would be loaded from the back and secured to the bed, but fully exposed to the slipstream (with all the dag incurred).  I suppose it would have to fly slower to reduce drag. During my Merchant Navy officer  training we had 2 weeks training in fire fighting in a ship superstructure built ashore for the purpose. So I KNOW what people just won't believe about the effects of smoke in an enclosed space.  Imagine being blindfolded and then  spun round half a dozen times,  but worse. You will become disorientated to the point of becoming lost even in a totally familiar environment. And the smoke can easily be so thick that even a bright light will be diffused to uselessness in inches. Even if the fire was in the same space, you may not be able to see anything but a diffused glow in its general direction.

At the end of the video you described some safety features that had been suggested, or implemented, and one of them was see-through blow up thing that went between the main instruments and the pilots viewing position and I thought that would not be such a good idea because it would provide extra complexity in a disaster situation and it may also be reliant on other systems, such as the one that provides pressure to the cockpit, also if seals were not good it could give more surfaces to get covered in smoke and grease reducing visibility, and in itself looks quite cumbersome. From what you've said, it appears to me that getting the smoke out the cockpit is really key so that pilots can stay in control. I had an idea. Imagine there was a small hole in the front of the cockpit in the bodywork,  forward facing, maybe the size of a small coin, that could be opened with a lever in emergency such as this, were the cabin has lost pressure but is filling with smoke. This would create some pressure in the cockpit as air hits the front of the plane and enters the cockpit and allow a through draft between this and the hole in the roof. It would be a really manual solution so there is less to go wrong, and would provide some redundancy. Obviously you would want to position this in such a way that it would not hit the pilots directly and perhaps put some sort of deflecting guard behind it to defuse airflow and deal with precipitation but I think it would help clear the air using the force of the wind and not be reliant on any other systems, it may be loud and create some turbulence in the cockpit but if it meant pilots could still see the controls and the pilots were not breathing in smoke, I think it potentially could be a life saver. Also if pilots can communicate on big headsets, with noise cancelling potential, the noise issue could be mitigated. that's an easy problem.  I like easy problems.  Put, a system in the cargo hold that floods it with an inert gas.  Nitrogen or Argon.  There are no people down there, so there is no issue with anybody suffocating.  It's a pretty inexpensive fix.  It will work 100%, with a zero percentage chance of failure.  It's an easy fix.  The only.  ONLY, reason for a failure on my system would be.  The owners actually wanted it to fail. It don't take no roads scholar to fix this issue. And, as a bonus.  Even the cargo will be saved.  Inert gas will not harm anything (except biological critters).  But pets and such won't be in that hold.

I note that their was a 3rd battery fire in 2016. What frightens me is thatL  1)The battery manufacturer voluntarily ceased manufacture and stated that the type of battery Boeing insisted on 'are inherently unsafe'.  2)Boeings initial response was to put the battery into a thicker metal box  3)Boeing has now replaced the battery for a less efficient non-rechargeable type.  Consider point 3 - the ONLY time that the battery would be used is when all other power fails.... so Boeing tried to cut overall cost by slightly reducing weigh (metal box foiled that) and IF their was a total power failure, the battery could be recharged.  That speaks or really bad design that is 100% aimed at reducing cost and since 'self certification' is in operation - NOBODY said 'that seems like a bad idea.  It strikes me that we will not see a group of hull losses due to a repeated failure BUT because all manner of rarely encountered conditions that the design has not considered and not adhered to the 'belt and braces' approach.  Of course, the crew are the biggest lifesavers and best resource on any aircraft but we did see pilots who were caught out by the AOA sensor failures. 2 sensors... 2. If you have 3, you can 'vote' on which to trust but 2.... and 2 that are used alternately? I suppose it saves the cost of 1 sensor... or, and the sensor that detects that the 2 inputs are different was an OPTION.  Airbus managed the long supply lines because they have experience of such stretching back to 1960 and because of the aircraft involved, cost was not the MOST important factor - getting it RIGHT was the most important factor.  I WOULD fly Boeing - but only with CVaptain Petter Hornfeldt in command. He's quite young but he is 'old school'. He has pride in getting it right.

There are two types of lithium batteries.  Lithium metal, which are non-rechargeable (CR-123's, Energizer ultimate lithium), and lithium ion rechargeables.  The lithium metal batteries contain lithium metal.   To extinguish a Lithium metal fire, you cannot use water based extinguishers.  Water mixed with lithium metal creates hydrogen and will make the fire worse... Extinguishers used for lithium metal are graphite, copper powder, and Lith-X (Ansul), or Class D type extinguishers.    Lithium Ion batteries do not contain lithium metal, and fires can be extinguished with standard ABC type extinguishers.    In my opinion, these two types of batteries should not be in the same cargo hold if there is fire suppression for one or the other.

Not having an effective vapor and temperature sensors in the cargo compartments; not having Halon units directly attached to sealed and "fire-proof" containers designed to monitor the unit and discharge automatically and alert the flight deck; not to place responsibility to the aircraft manufacturer to include those measures in their liability clauses, and put it into statute law for conversions, and the insurance companies plans; all are criminal in my mind. To hear it prattled about that the costs aren't statistically justified, is moral and ethical criminality, either denied, or to be passed along to the next sub-contractor, aircraft user's liability insurance. While not easily accomplished, is it possible to modify the cockpit, whereby it is environmentally isolated from the rest of the the cabin to vent out smoke, and lower oxygen content for fire suppression? Being able to isolate the flight deck, vent the smoke, have more than 20 minutes of supplemental oxygen, on both passenger and cargo, may allow the crew to avoid further casualties on the ground should they fail.

Just to add a correction @ 5:03 MP incorrectly refers to the Persian Gulf as the Southern Arabian Gulf. For anybody interested, ancient geographers such as Strabo and Ptolemy also referred to this stretch of water as the Persian Gulf. However, in the 1960s due to the rise of nationalism states, some Arab‚Äôs began calling it Arabian Gulf with the hope and intent to alter the official name. So please MP, for future reference, use the correct name: Persian Gulf.

For those of us involved in model aviation, we have used Lithium batteries almost from their first introduction to the commercial market.  Many of us have seen first hand what can happen when these batteries go into Thermal Runaway.  Given the sheer numbers of these cells that were being dense packed and shipped on pallets, the fact this accident happened is not a surprise.  The real surprise is that it didn't happen much sooner.  Lithium Polymer cells are particularly at risk of a case rupture and fire with even relatively minor external damage.  What is even more unbelievable is that even after this fire and crash, Boeing put these batteries in the Dream Liner with the equally predictable results.  Boeing's after the fact solution?  Build a steel case fire place with a chimney and put the batteries in it!  It's so sad so many regulations have to be literally written in blood.

I would implement sound guidance for some instruments' input too.
You can eliminate lithium battery fire risk by discharging the battery. That's because the battery is a self-contained fuel cell, with fuel and oxidizer sealed inside. At 100% charge, they're fully separated and the chemical potential is the greatest. At 0% charge, they're fully reacted so it has no chemical potential and cannot ignite at all. It's like setting fire to a brick of coal vs a brick of ash.

Not trying to be critical.  There is no mention that the reason for not allowing Li-po batteries in the cargo holds is the delayed reaction time in case of self-combusting.The delay is greatly reduced when the battery are stored within close proximity such as the over head compartment. I would also add an advice for charging before a flight. Do not charge your batteries in devices or powerbanks full. Probability of fire is greatly reduced if you are at 80% vs 100%. For powerbanks or spare batteries (like for drones) I would even go lower, like 30-70% depending (70% if you need to use during flight,  30-40% if not). And freight cargo I suppose they did follow standard of 40% charge for storage (or maybe there was a mistake and it was higher) but probably if it was charged to only 10% fire would not broke out. Problem is if LiIon discharge to very low levels they should be charged with lower currents and if they are in packs individual cells can go negatif and they either should be disposed or charging circuitry will consider them unsafe (both cases a problem).

This is one of the most heartwrenching stories. The poor first officer up there alone helpless but fighting to the end. As for the poor Captain sadly he was probably dead shortly after getting up from his chair. I look forward to Peter talking about Airport Fire Rescue and some of the complexities involved. It's kind of refreshing to find a pilot that actually has some experience there and understands it. Most I've found never quite grasp the purpose of that infamous ATC question "How much Fuel and How many souls on board?". It helps the Rescue response gage needs. Fuel gives them an idea of how much foam they will need. And will they need to call in external Fire resources for more? Souls on board gives them a heads up for EMS response. Yeah that pilot that goes "Yah, maybe you should tell them to bring an ambulance to" is kind of oddly amusing. Much like the Pilots in the Cockpit the Fire Rescue has their own series of protocols procedures preplans and prayers  that kick in once ATC initiates the call.  One thing that made me curious. Why don't pilot Oxygen masks use the full face mask similar to a firefighters SCBA mask? The Advantage with them is they put the wearers entire face in a positive pressure bubble so toxic smoke cannot reach their mouth nose or eyes. And they are certainly no more a hinderance to pilot vision or performance than the archaic and idiotic smoke goggles. They also let you talk better as they can mount the mic farther away from the mouth. Along the similar vein why don't cargo aircraft have a Halon system?
I have had an inflight cockpit fire (electrical in origin) in a small aircraft (C-150), and fortunately it happened while I was about 1000 feet above the ground, in a landing approach at an airfield.  An electrical short rapidly developed into a small ball of fire behind the instrument panel on the copilot side and the cockpit filled with dense black smoke in a matter of seconds, to the point that I could not see or breathe.  By opening the window on the pilot side in order to see to land and to be able to breathe fanned the flames, but cleared enough of the smoke.  I landed and escaped with only a bit of smoke inhalation and a slightly singed arm.  That incident/accident was not something I would wish on anyone, but fortunately it is an exceptionally rare thing to have happen.  The aircraft was destroyed.  The key thing in an aircraft fire, especially a cabin fire, is to get it on the ground as soon as possible and then get away from the aircraft quickly...  Very sad that the UPS crew died, but the first officer saved many lives with his actions - my definition of a hero for sure.

Really sad.  This outstanding narrative brings up a few questions about firefighting, including the use of high altitude to try to starve a fire of oxygen.  With lithium batteries, unfortunately, a great deal of fire and smoke can be created with the energy that is stored in the battery chemistry.  LiIon and LiPo batteries cannot be fully discharged without damaging them, so they have to be shipped partly charged, and that chemical energy in a shorted battery will generate a lot of heat and smoke, with no oxygen and with not much effect from fire fighting chemicals.

Sad story, I'm sure it contributed to the reason why many batteries now have an auto discharge feature after few days, so they are shipped in discharged state.
I used to sell mobile phones at the time and shipping regulations changed massively from this. Before it, there was zero checks on lithium and sellers in China wouldn't declare them as such for tax reasons (service to buyers). In the months that followed there was a moratorium on air freight of lithium battery  containing devices and after the new rules came up we have to declare content and have extra fees for lithium. Might be some insurance, not sure. If I remember correctly, they also try to spread them out in the cargo as well. I remember orders being split on different planes. All in all I saw the change with my own eyes, it was swift and effective. People sometimes complain about it when boarding planes, but I always remember why it is so important. Great video.

What do you mean that the aircraft had no fire extinguishing system but it had a fire suppression system which is activated through the use of the non normal checklist? Also, would the use of the carbon dioxide extinguishing system used by chemical industries work here? If you just release gas into the cargohold, pressure will build up. However if there is a controlled release, the carbon dioxide can flush away the oxygen for a few minutes. Once the cardon dioxide runs out, the cargohold can be resealed in hopes of denying the fire oxygen (which would need the fire liner to be intact to work, but it helps with any other fire). On one hand, the industrial systems are heavy and cumbersome, so they might end up weighting more than the cargo, but I suspect a lot of the weight is not the containers storing the carbon dioxide, but the plumbling, after all handheld units aren't that heavy and I think if you had 3000 times the CO2 of a handheld unit, you'd have a decent chance of smothering out the entire cargo hold. Or maybe I'm wrong and such a system would be way too heavy to be practical.

Engineers made a lot to isolate pilots from cargo area - they made isolated pressure systems, they made a vent door for cargo area to "suffocate" fire, they added dedicated smoke removal vent to the cabin to suck out smoke when cabin is pressurized. But one switch that turned off cabin pressure system ruined all the efforts, to me it was turned off by mistake in a hurry or something like that.  Some ideas that came to my mind: 1) turning something off (like cabin pressure system) should get more attention, maybe some additional action should be required, like turning Reverse in many car transmissions requires you to pay more attention; 2) I don't know, maybe that there is a sound system that beeps on every turn of nobs, like heading selector, it would slightly help when you see nothing. In school when there was no touchscreens we could write an SMS under the table by knowing what letter we needed and how many clicks we needed to get this letter. 3) Special smoke mode. don't know whether it would help or whether it possible to implement but several times more light for instruments in cabin also might be helpful, like the rear fog lights in cars, which are very bright (at clear night they can blind a car behind) and allowed to be turned on only in fog.  It should be big weird-shaped button (to easily recognize by touch in darkness) that lights everything in cabin like a Christmas tree.
No matter how many times I hear about this incident it manages to wrench my heart.

On September 3rd 2010, I witnessed the crash of an UPS B747 in Dubai. This tragic aviation event that is well documented in the video.  The sun had set about an hour earlier, when a UPS cargo B747-400 airliner, crashed at 19.42h just outside Silicon Oasis, the place I had been living at for a number of years.   I can vividly remember how an aircraft came over the top of my house at barely a few hundred feet while I was reading something in the living room just after dinner. The noise was clearly not that of a typical airliner overflying the house. With idle thrust, the majority was all airframe noise. Very unusual.   The next second I found myself standing in my front door opening, witnessing a large 4 engine airliner flying away from me in a straight line, to crash and burst into flames just moments later.   It looks like that the final reaction for FO Bell to disconnect the AP to reduce the rate of descent less than a minute before impact, was the one thing that saved my own and many others lifes in the neighbourhood. We will never know if this was done to avoid the large residential area, nor does it really matter.  Captain Doug Lampe and First-Officer Matthew Bell, even though we never met, you will always be remembered by the Dubai Silicon Oasis pilot community at the time. No September 3rd passes without thinking about you both. RIP fellow Aviators.

RIP Matthew Bell....

So much of this reflects the Swissair 111 inflight fire disaster. Rapidly escalating out of reach fire overtakes even the best trained crews. The Swissair of course was much worse as it was a passenger flight and all perished. It just has to be re-said - the first sign of fire Land Land Land. Forget looking up procedure manuals and fire check lists - valuable minutes can be lost - seconds count in this situation  -  First Priority is to get the aircraft on the ground - even if it means off-airport in such extreme cases as this . Not sure if it would have made any difference for the UPS crew to have gone direct to Bahrain (100 miles) rather than turn back to Dubai (180 miles) But it sure may have given them a better chance before they and the aircraft became completely incapacitated. Lithium batteries should be blanket banned as aircraft cargo. If passengers smoking can be banned many years ago, lithium batteries present a million times more risk being carried as cargo in out of reach location.

All kudos to the memory of those professional pilots, they fought to the end. That the aviation industry could suggest some cheap little blow-up is an insult to their memory, as is the proposal to make all commercial aircraft have only ONE pilot [yes and automatics]. Human are fragile lumps of vulnerable protoplasm, their logic systems can be as buggy as hell. All that said, they are better than AI, and always will be.  Crews on cargo aircraft [particularly] need to be increased, not decreased.  They need a flight engineer, or crew chief, or cargo chief. Someone who can don a portable air supply, and protective clothing and go LOOK.  Military aircraft and ships [apart from some fighter aircraft, and I suppose, some midget submarines] have a crew. People on lookout, people who keep watch and "patrol the perimeter". The pilots have to try to fly the aircraft, and as we can see, under extremely adverse and worsening conditions, tension-filled, and in this event, impossible to prevail. One of them leaving the flight deck to actually get first-hand information would not have worked either. Not an expert, but it would have probably broken several laws, as well as being against company policy and procedures for the situation.  A thousand fire extinguishers might not have helped, as well being expensive and reducing payload. In passenger aircraft the flight attendants are trained to some extent, on helping the flight deck. They act as eyes and ears, if nothing else, and can back-up, or confirm or deny, what the instrumentation is telling the pilots. But a well-trained human with air and PPE, and a fire extinguisher? [Suitable for all fire types]. It seems like a plan to me, though I am not flight crew, cabin crew, or fire fighter.  The armed forces [despite rumours to the contrary] try to train their people to be flexible and competent in a range of skills. In combat, or in an emergency, you can't bank on a specialist surviving or being competent. Hence army medics, but everyone learns first aid. Everyone learns how to use the radios, and so on. On ships sailors of all ranks learn the rudiments about almost everything, especially the fundamentals on how to run the ship.  In the military you are trained to start your day, perhaps under harsh, often dangerous environmental conditions, with the possibly or or certainty that someone will be shooting at you [in times of war], and be able to sustain that for considerable periods of time. Going to "the office" which is uncomfortable, just to start your job. Commercial aviation is safe, except when it is not. On those rare occasions you need spare capacity, spare human capacity to work the unexpected problem. Automatics are aids, not replacements for human skill and ingenuity.   A human "robot" is the ultimate Swizz army knife.  It's heuristics are all about survival. A blow-up plastic cockpit hood? Seriously folks?

An interesting thing to note about this incident is that right after it had occurred, when the only thing that was still known about the matter at the time was that the plane crashed due to an onboard fire, UPS voluntarily started their own serious & fullfledged investigation into trying to find ways to improve the fire-prevention/safety systems onboard their aircrafts. UPS started doing this prior to the completion of the air crash investigation and release of the final report, so hats off to them for taking such initiative. I'll also add that they were also among the first companies to both take up the recommendations made by the related investigation team (shortly after the final report's publication), and implement new fire-related measures (like EVAS).

Why couldn't the cockpit windows be opened so that the smoke could escape due to the pressure difference at altitude and in the cockpit? Below 10,000 feet, a person can breathe comfortably.
There cannot possibly be a worse situation a pilot can face than an on-board fire. There are other situations that can make an aircraft very difficult to handle but chances are with some skill and luck the pilots may be able to find a way to control the aircraft. With an on-board fire, it is only a matter of time before all controls are rendered useless. This would probably happen very fast so even the pilots might go from full control for several minutes where they are confident they could probably fly the plane to complete lack of control within seconds. And there is simply no way to escape a fire in a tube. And what makes it even more difficult that the smoke would overwhelm the crew before the fire renders the controls unusable. If I was to write instructions on how to handle an on-board fire I would advise that if the nearest airport is over 20 minutes away look to land anywhere immediately.

Small Lithium battery ... put it on a glass or metal container with a lid. A vacuum bottle type coffee pot is great... if it fits.  At home, put it in a pot that has a lid which fits tightly or a "dutch oven" with heavy cast lid.  Place on a heat resistant surface. Placing in the sink is usually good.  DO NOT USE WATER  It will continue to burn, but you are containing the heat and smoke.  You can also cover it in sand, but that is less likely to be available.  Appx 5 min, it's done.

You made me cry this time.  I was a submariner and I fly high performance RC aircraft.  Imagine a fire on a sub.  Same situation.  I've had a misbalanced LiPo fail and detonate just as I got it outside. (5 cell 3000mAh, 4 cell primary failure, 15ft fireball according to witness mom, 5th cell went 1 minute later was out of range then)  FYI people, the non removable battery in your cell phone is held together with aluminum foil practically.  If your phone or any other device is bulging I highly discourage you from ever flying with it.  If any of your devices with internal batteries bulge in the slightest, replace them as soon as possible and dispose of them at a proper location.  Hobbyist trick if you cannot dispose of it properly in time, drop the device into a large bucket of salt water OUTSIDE at least 10 feet from anything flammable.  Let the salt water discharge the battery for at least one month or longer if you know it was at full charge. (at least 1 week)  IF THE BATTERY IS PUNCTURED DO NOT DROP IN WATER!  LITHIUM WILL BASICALLY DETONATE WHEN EXPOSED TO WATER!  If your battery is punctured, place it outside as far away from anything as you can and do not approach it for a week at least.  After this, either waiting for a puncture to become inert (atmospheric water absorption) or discharging in salt water (discharging electrolyte beyond recovery) to become inert, then you can take the battery to a local recycling center.  Technically, now being inert, it could be disposed of in the refuse but we need to preserve all the lithium we have for future generations energy storage needs so please recycle responsibly. They give off serious heat, flammable toxic smoke, sparks and jet's of flame. It's almost impossible to put them out once they ignite as the reaction is self oxidising, and water just makes the stuff even more angry. Once they're alight the best thing you can do is just stay away from them, unfortunately, on an aircraft in flight that isn't really an option.  When an electric car catches fire, the most emergency services can usually do is keep people safe, prevent the fire from spreading to other nearby things, and wait for it to finally burn itself out (.......... My advice to pilots; If you detect smoke, either through smell or an alarm, immediately head for the closest airport with an acceptable runway.     Once you've done that, then you can second guess your senses or the alarm. You can try to find the cause or dance a jig. If you find the situation is not an emergency, then just cancel the landing request and get back on course.        The important thing is to not let anything get in the way of landing that aircraft A.S.A.P!      I don't care if where you're landing has no fire fighting ability. The clock is ticking and I'd rather land sooner and get everybody out, than lower my chance of survival by flying longer to a better equipped airport.       I am in no way criticizing these pilots. Their deaths, and other fire deathsk helped me reach the above opinion.       The normalcy bias has killed hundreds of airline passengers.       If it's a false alarm, your passengers and cargo are alive. If it's a real fire, you have vastly increased your chances of survival.      Don't f__k with fire!

At work we commonly ship Class 8 corrosive goods. Have been asked several times not to declare it because of the costs involved.   It would be very easy to loose awareness of what other major airports are in the area.
I've always wondered what is a safe way to carry Li-ion batteries on an aircraft. If a Li-ion battery undergoes thermal runaway failure, that will become a class delta fire (you indirectly mentioned this when you said that it creates its own oxygen). Class delta fires cannot be extinguished with handheld extinguishers, because even if you use CO2 to starve the fire of oxygen, the fuel itself is producing enough oxygen to sustain the fire. Given this is there ANY safe way to carry a Li-ion battery aboard a plane? Every passenger has one in their pocket.

Might be worth emphasizing a point made in this video, about batteries.  Lithium ion batteries aren't just a flammable material, containing fuel that can burn. Most lithium ion batteries also contain the oxidizer necessary to sustain combustion, meaning that a fire can start deep within a battery assembly or sealed container, where there is very little air or none at all. Finally, they also contain the energy needed to start a fire -- electrochemical energy which can be instantly converted to heat if the battery is shorted, damaged internally, or enters a thermal runaway condition. Lithium ion batteries are literally all three sides of the "fire triangle" you might have learned about in elementary school, all in one package. (Lithium iron phosphate batteries in particular, it should be noted, are an exception -- they have a significantly reduced tendency to self-ignite.)
So, if we do as instructed and keep lithium batteries in our carry on luggage... how does cabin crew handle a fire in our carry on luggage? Or in a cellphone or computer?

Am wondering why  Dubai controllers could not or did not tune to Bahrain frequencies to communicate with emergency aircraft. Thank you for covering this event. I always remember it very solemnly. I am a ramp employee at UPS at the main hub in Louisville Kentucky. I remember watching the aircraft, N571UP, as it departed Louisville for what would be the last time, just a couple of days before the accident. The mood at UPS was very solemn: it was the first time a UPS plane had gone down with loss of life. Since then UPS has indeed taken many measures to insure a similar accident doesn't happen again. Improved oxygen masks for flight crew. Fire resistant containers and coverings for pallets which are now in widespread use. And tighter regulations for carrying lithium batteries abord aircraft. This accident and the investigation made flying both freight and passengers much safer.  On a side note, N571UP was a young 747. At the time of the accident, the aircraft was just three years old, and had just over 10,000 hours flying time.

It would probably be difficult to design a fire suppression system for those cargo areas because you don't know what kind of fire you would be encountering.  Offhand, I think you might have a system that would fill the compartment with an inert gas like nitrogen, but you don't know if there might be some kind of a fire that could combine with nitrogen to form something bad.  The other way is to deprive the fire of oxygen, even a lithium fire that provides his own oxygen, by maybe pulling a partial vacuum on the cargo area and pumping the gas into the atmosphere via one way valve.  That would probably mitigate even even a lithium fire, because you would eject any oxygen that it made.  The other thing that might help would be if you had an installed system and possibly a couple portable handheld systems in the cockpit that would draw in smoke-filled air, filter out the smoke and eject clear air into the cockpit,  something like a charcoal filter device with a fan in it.

If you buy into my theory that pack 1 running might've avoided that control failure (not to mention the failure in the oxygen system) or at least made it less severe, AND it turns out that pack 1 had not been running simply due to crew error (either turning it off or failing to reset it) then you could argue that the captain's decision to take and retain control of the aircraft was probably the moment that its fate was decided; the captain could certainly have made the same mistake, but it seems FAR less likely at his level of experience. I am not a chemist, but I think the term "on fire" or "catching fire" is a bit misleading when it comes to batteries.   What we laymen usually refer to as fire is actually just the rapid oxygenation of something. Carbon in tree, cloth, oil reacting and combining with oxygen to create say CO2. In a chemical sense, iron that is rusting is also on fire since it is also reacting and combing with oxygen.  What goes in lithium batteries and other batteries too, is more like what happens in an electric heater. In a heater the wire gets warm when electricity flows through it. Same with a battery, except there is no external wire. The electricity flows inside the broken battery, inside through what is an internal, electrical short. And unlike a heater where the resistance in the wire limits the amount of electric current that can flow through it, there is hardly any resistance inside a broken battery. Thus the stored electricity is released at a much higher speed, and the battery gets extremely hot. And now a chain reaction follows that causes any nearby battery to heat up, fail, short out, heat up,... Eventually packing materials, the crate, etc catches (real) fire.  As can be deducted from the above, standard fire suppression systems have little effect on battery "fires" since they work by quenching fire by removing or limiting the amount of oxygen that is available to feed the fire/oxygenation. Pouring water on such a "fire" will not do much, except for lowering the temperature due to the cooling effect of evaporation of the water. To stop a run-away battery fire in a electric car, the recommendation is to pour lots of water over it until it has consumed itself, or (better yet) lift the whole burning car into a giant bathtub, fill it with water, and cool it down and stop the chain reaction.

It won't help for anything like shippers, but if you're carrying a battery not contained in a device, discharge it as fully as you're able (to safe levels) before transporting it. Overdischarging is bad too, but a fully charged cell is much more dangerous from a fire perspective.  BigClive has done some videos discussing this and other YouTubers have shown that discharged batteries are much less likely to fail catastrophically.

This is so sad. In 2019 I had to fly within the US and I had my vape with me. That uses high-draw (30 Amp) lithium polymer batteries. It ended up powered off in my backpack in my control the entire time. That being said, I have had an 18650 battery start venting and it is a very violent process. It also produces quite noxious fumes. I am honestly not sure how one would handle a runaway chemical process like that inside an aircraft. I mean, if we think back to Richard Hammond's crash in the Rimac electric car, they had that vehicle on the ground, in an environment where they could do whatever they wanted, use whatever processes and tools they wanted, but that chain reaction took 5 days to burn itself out.
